            "msg_body": "Yesterday I mentioned the system that we teach in our BAS Scoping and Estimating beta which is closing today.\nIn this email I am going to take you through this system (side note for those of you who are not estimators or sales people this system can still help you to organize your projects and designs)\nThis system assumes that you have some prerequisite knowledge around BAS and IT (which we teach in the course). \nIf you think about a time when estimating was hard, when the scope just didn't seem to come it's probably hard to pin down why. Why was it so difficult to creating a simple scope of work and estimate?\nIt has to do with all of the moving parts and pieces that are found in a construction design.\nAfter working with hundreds of sales people and estimators I learned that each person would approach their estimating and design differently. But I also started to identify patterns. \nThe one's who seemed to get their designs done effortlessly started with the documentation. \nNow I realize this isn't some super sexy idea but it is important.\nIf you've ever created a design only to realize you were working off the wrong or incomplete documents then you know how important this task is.\nSo step one: Identify the current and correct documents for the project\nFrom here we need to process the information within these documents. This is another fork in the proverbial road of scoping and estimating. \nIt is here that most people go straight for the equipment schedule and start counting equipment. This is a good strategy but it is not the best strategy. \nAt this point the great estimators and sales persons step back and look at the job and take the scope in \"context\". \nIs this a project where one half of the building is being retrofit? \nWhat does that potentially mean to the scope? \nAre there parts and pieces that can be leveraged? \nAre there risks that need to be avoided?\nCan you see how not understanding the context of the project could result in a massively different design? Imagine if you included a server, graphics, and all of the associated setup in your project only to realize you were using the existing campus server?\nStep two: Understand the context of the project\nWith the context thoroughly understood the rest of the process becomes rather \"mechanical\". It is simply a series of steps that executed in the correct sequence result in scopes and estimates that are spot on. \nQuite simply you (I know this doesn't look simple but it is, trust me):\nEvaluate the Equipment Schedule for an overall system count\nList out the sub components for those systems\nReview the specification to ensure that your sub components adhere to specification requirements\nUtilize the RFI/RFC process to close any gaps between your sub components and the specified sub components\nReview your completed sub-component list and build out the higher level bill of materials\nIdentify and validate labor and subcontractor costs\nCreate your scope of work and proposal\nEvaluate the compete scope and estimate and chose a competitive margin/markup strategy\nStep three therefore is to scope and estimate the project\nThis step should actually be the easiest step in the whole process.\nWatch this short video below to see the process.\nWhile this video was short, it captures the essence of understanding the project and identifying potential areas of scope creep as well as risk.\nThis is what you will learn when you enroll in the BAS Scoping and Estimating Beta.
